d><td>22.9</td></tr><tr><td>2-C</td><td>0.0150</td><td>317.0</td><td>12.04</td><td>0.38</td><td>0.997</td><td>0.501</td><td>24.0</td></tr><tr><td>2-D</td><td>0.0200</td><td>317.1</td><td>12.55</td><td>0.38</td><td>0.996</td><td>0.500</td><td>25.1</td></tr><tr><td>2-E</td><td>0.0300</td><td>317.4</td><td>13.51</td><td>0.39</td><td>0.994</td><td>0.499</td><td>27.1</td></tr><tr><td>2-F</td><td>0.0301</td><td>317.8</td><td>13.55</td><td>0.39</td><td>0.994</td><td>0.499</td><td>27.2</td></tr><tr><td>2-G</td><td>0.0500</td><td>318.4</td><td>15.45</td><td>0.40</td><td>0.988</td><td>0.494</td><td>31.3</td></tr><tr><td>2-H</td><td>0.0699</td><td>319.0,</td><td>17.21</td><td>0.41</td><td>0.980</td><td>0.489</td><td>35.2</td></tr><tr><td>3-A</td><td>0.1005</td><td>119.4</td><td>7.88</td><td>0.48</td><td>0.977</td><td>0.225</td><td>35.3</td></tr><tr><td>3-B</td><td>0.2040</td><td>120.9</td><td>11.10</td><td>0.47</td><td>0.934</td><td>0.212</td><td>52.3</td></tr><tr><td>3-C</td><td>0.0540</td><td>120.1</td><td>6.36</td><td>0.46</td><td>0.990</td><td>0.225</td><td>28.3</td></tr><tr><td>3-D</td><td>0.0137</td><td>120.1</td><td>5.11</td><td>0.43</td><td>0.997</td><td>0.227</td><td>22.5</td></tr><tr><td>3-E</td><td>0.1506</td><td>121.2</td><td>9.55</td><td>0.45</td><td>0.958</td><td>0.216</td><td>44.2</td></tr></table>

# APPENDIX C

# PRECISION AND ERROR ANALYSIS

The general form of the equation used to determine the thermal conductivity in this investigation is Eq. (9), rearranged as

$$
k = x \left\{\frac {Q _ {i} ^ {\prime} / A}{\left[ \left(\frac {\Delta T}{F}\right) _ {X} - \left(\frac {\Delta T}{F}\right) _ {O} \right]} - \frac {1 6}{3} \overline {{n}} ^ {2} \sigma T ^ {3} \left(\frac {Y _ {T , E}}{T}\right) _ {X} \right\}, \tag {C-1}
$$

where the measured heat flux, $Q^{\prime} / A$ , is assumed to be constant during the measurements and the specimen thickness is $x \equiv \Delta x$ .

By taking the total derivative of Eq. (C-1) and rearranging terms, the change in $k$ due to a change in any of the quantities in Eq. (C-1) is

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {\mathrm {d} k}{k} = \frac {\mathrm {d} x}{x} + \frac {Q ^ {\prime} / A}{J K} \left[ \frac {\mathrm {d} \left(Q ^ {\prime} / A\right)}{Q ^ {\prime} / A} \right] - \frac {Q ^ {\prime} / A}{J K ^ {2}} \left(\frac {\Delta T}{F}\right) _ {x} \left(\frac {\mathrm {d} \Delta T _ {x}}{\Delta T _ {x}} - \frac {\mathrm {d} F _ {x}}{F _ {x}}\right) + \tag {C-2} \\ \frac {Q ^ {\prime} / A}{J K ^ {2}} \left(\frac {\Delta T}{F}\right) _ {O} \left(\frac {d \Delta T _ {o}}{\Delta T _ {o}} - \frac {d F _ {o}}{F _ {o}}\right) - \frac {L}{J} \left[ \frac {2 d n}{n} + \frac {3 d T}{T} + \frac {d (Y / \tau)}{Y / \tau} \right] \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$J =$ quantity enclosed by braces in Eq. (C-1),

$\mathbf{K} =$ quantity enclosed by brackets in Eq. (C-1), and

$$
L = (1 6 / 3) \overline {{n}} ^ {2} \sigma T ^ {3} (Y / \tau) _ {X}.
$$

By using certain approximations, Eq. (C-2) can be simplified to express quantities more readily measured,

$$
w \equiv \left[ \frac {\Delta T}{F (Q ^ {\prime} / A)} \right] _ {O},
$$

$$
h _ {r} = \frac {1 6}{3} \frac {\overline {{n ^ {2}}}}{\varepsilon} \sigma T ^ {3} (Y / \tau) _ {X} = 4 n ^ {2} \sigma T ^ {3},
$$

and

$$
\Delta \mathrm {T} _ {\mathrm {x}} = \Delta \mathrm {T} _ {\mathrm {s x}} + \Delta \mathrm {T} _ {\mathrm {o}},
$$

$$
\left(\triangle \mathrm {T} _ {\mathrm {S}} / \mathrm {F}\right) _ {\mathrm {X}} = \frac {\mathrm {Q} ^ {\prime} / \mathrm {A}}{\left(\mathrm {k} / \mathrm {x}\right) + \varepsilon \mathrm {h} _ {\mathrm {r}}}
$$

$$
T = k x
$$

In the present studies, the heat shunting was usually small so that $F_{X}$ and $F_{O}$ can be assumed to be unity in the weighting functions. Furthermore, the error in $Y_{\tau, e} / \tau$ can be evaluated as

$$
\frac {d (Y / \tau)}{Y / \tau} = \left( \begin{array}{l l} \tau & d Y \\ Y & d \tau \end{array} - 1\right) \left( \begin{array}{l} \overline {{d _ {\kappa}}} \\ \overline {{\kappa}} \end{array} + \frac {d x}{x}\right) + \frac {e}{Y} \frac {d Y}{d \varepsilon} \frac {d e}{e}.
$$

Finally, the total error in the conductivity would be approximately,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {d k}{k} = \frac {d x}{x} + \left[ \left(1 + e h _ {r} x\right) / k\right) \left\{\frac {d \left(Q ^ {\prime} / A\right)}{Q ^ {\prime} / A} - \frac {d \Delta T _ {S X}}{\Delta T _ {S X}} + \frac {d F _ {x}}{F _ {x}} + w \left(k / x\right) + e h _ {r} \right] x \\ \left(\frac {d F _ {x}}{F _ {x}} - \frac {d F _ {0}}{F _ {0}}\right) - \left(\frac {\varepsilon h _ {r} x}{k + \varepsilon h _ {r} x}\right) \left[ \binom {2 d n} {\bar {n}} + \frac {3 d T}{T} \right] + \left(\frac {\tau}{Y} \frac {d Y}{d \tau} - 1\right) \times \tag {C-3} \\ \left. \left(\frac {\mathrm {d} \bar {\kappa}}{\kappa} + \frac {\mathrm {d x}}{\mathrm {x}}\right) + \left( \begin{array}{c c c} \varepsilon & \mathrm {d Y} & \mathrm {d e} \\ - & \dots & \dots \\ \mathrm {Y} & \mathrm {d e} & \varepsilon \end{array} \right) \right] \Bigg \} \\ \end{array}
$$

The magnitude or the error in the conductivity is dependent on the specimen thickness. Some of the uncertainties increase and others decrease with a change in specimen thickness. Therefore, there should be an optimum thickness that would minimize the errors associated with it. The errors are also dependent on the magnitude of the thermal conductivity, the fixed resistance, and $\varepsilon h_{\mathrm{r}}$ .

The error in the conductivity measurements now considered refers to the apparatus model. III-B for various values of x, k, eh, and $\Delta T / F(Q' / A)$ observed in the present studies.

Using the accuracy limits listed in Table 2 and the tolerance limits shown in Figs. A-1 and A-2, the error terms appearing in Eq. (C-3) can be determined as follows:

# Temperature level

$\mathrm{dT} / \mathrm{T} = \pm 0.2\%$ (published error for Pt vs Pt-10% Rh thermocouple) The potentiometer and other temperature measuring errors can be neglected in comparison with the thermocouple error.

# Temperature differences

$$
\mathrm {d} \Delta \mathrm {T} = (\Delta \mathrm {T} / \mathrm {T}) \mathrm {d T}, \text {or} \mathrm {d} \Delta \mathrm {T} / \Delta \mathrm {T} = \mathrm {d T} / \mathrm {T} = \pm 0.2 \% (\mathrm {Pt} \text {vs} \mathrm {Pt}-10 \% \text {Rh thermocouple})
$$

# Measured heat flux

$$
Q ^ {\prime} / A = 4 \mathrm {E V I} / (\pi D ^ {2}) = 4 \mathrm {E V} (V _ {1} / \Omega) / (\pi D ^ {2}),
$$

$$
\frac {\mathrm {d} \left(\mathrm {Q} _ {6} ^ {\prime} / \mathrm {A}\right)}{\mathrm {Q} _ {6} ^ {\prime} / \mathrm {A}} = \frac {\mathrm {d E}}{\mathrm {E}} + \frac {\mathrm {d V}}{\mathrm {V}} - \frac {\mathrm {d} \Omega}{\Omega} - \frac {2 \mathrm {d D}}{\mathrm {D}} + \frac {\mathrm {d V} _ {1}}{\mathrm {V} _ {1}},
$$

$$
\mathrm {d V} / \mathrm {V} = \mathrm {d V} _ {1} / \mathrm {V} _ {1} = \pm (1 0) (0. 0 1) = \pm 0. 1
$$

$$
\mathrm{d}\Omega /\Omega = \pm 0.04\% ,
$$

$$
\mathrm {d D} / \mathrm {D} = \pm (0. 0 0 5 \times 1 0 0) / 3. 2 5 = \pm 0. 1 5,
$$

$$
\begin{aligned} \mathrm{dE} / \mathrm{E} & = \pm (0.25\times 100) / 60 = 0.42\% \\ & = \pm 0.25\text{in.}) \end{aligned}
$$

Another uncertainty in the heat flux is the axial heat loss across the air gap between the main and guard heaters:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \left(\mathrm {Q} / \mathrm {A}\right) _ {\mathrm {L}} = \left(\frac {\mathrm {k} _ {\mathrm {a i r}}}{\mathrm {x}} + \frac {\varepsilon_ {\mathrm {P t h}}}{3}\right) \Delta \mathrm {T} \\ = \left(6. 3 k _ {a i r} + 0. 0 3 h _ {r}\right) \Delta T a n d d (Q / A) _ {L} = \left(6. 3 k _ {a i r} + 0. 0 3 h _ {r}\right) d \Delta T \\ \end{array}
$$

but

$$
d \Delta T = T d T / T;
$$

therefore,

$$
\frac{\mathrm{d}(\mathrm{Q / A})_{\mathrm{L}}}{\mathrm{Q}^{\prime} / \mathrm{A}} = \left(\frac{6.3\mathrm{K}_{\text{air}} + 0.03\mathrm{h}_{\mathrm{r}}}{\mathrm{Q}^{\prime} / \mathrm{A}}\right)\mathrm{T}\left(\pm 0.20\%\right)
$$

Finally,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac{\mathrm{d}\left(\mathrm{Q}^{\prime} / \mathrm{A}\right)}{\mathrm{Q}^{\prime} / \mathrm{A}} = (\pm 0.42\%) + (\pm 0.2\%) - (\pm 0.04\%) - (\pm 0.30\%) \\ + \frac {\left(6.3 k _ {a i r} + 0.03 h _ {r}\right)}{Q ^ {\prime} / A} T (\pm 0.20 \%) \\ \end{array}
$$

Average refractive index

$$
\begin{array}{l} \overline {{d n}} / \overline {{n}} = \pm (0. 2 \times 1 0 0) / 1. 5 = \pm 1 3 \\ = 0.0\% \left(\overline{\mathrm{n}} = 1.000 \text{for argon and helium}\right). \\ \end{array}
$$

Radiation function

The expression for $\mathrm{Y} / \tau$ is quite complex [Eq. (8)]; fortunately, for $\varepsilon = 0.5$ and $\tau < 5$ , $\mathrm{Y} / \tau$ is very nearly a constant and for $\tau > 10$ is very nearly equal to $1 / \tau$ . Thus, the error in $\mathrm{Y} / \tau$ due to an uncertainty in $\tau$ can be neglected, except in the narrow interval $5 < \tau > 10$ . Furthermore, the maximum value of the term $\varepsilon \mathrm{dY} / \mathrm{Yd}\varepsilon \cong 1$ , so that the error in $\mathrm{Y} / \tau$ is primarily due to the uncertainty in the surface emissivity of the upper and lower plates.

Surface emissivity

$$
\mathrm{d}\varepsilon /\varepsilon = \pm (0.05\times 100) / 0.45 = \pm 11.1\% (\text{estimated from the vacuum})
$$

Specimen thickness

Dial indicator:

$$
\mathrm {d} x / x = \pm (0. 0 0 0 0 5) (2. 5 4) \times 1 0 0 / x = \pm 0. 0 1 3
$$

Thermal expansion:

Although a fused quartz rod was used to minimize thermal expansion effects in the mobile piston assembly, it was not practical to make a dual quartz rod dial indicator system. The uncertainty caused by the thermal expansion difference of stainless steel from that of fused quartz is estimated to be

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {d} x / x = \pm 0. 5 [ \alpha L (\Delta T _ {\mathrm {S X}} / 2) ] = \pm 0. 5 [ (2 0 \times 1 0 ^ {- 6}) (2 5) / 2 ] \Delta T _ {\mathrm {S X}} \\ = \pm 0.013 T _ {s x} \% \text {or} d x / x = \pm 0.013 \left[ \left(Q ^ {\prime} / A\right) / k + \varepsilon h _ {r} x \right] \% \\ \end{array}
$$

where

$$
\alpha = \text {c o e f f i c i e n t} ^ {\prime} \text {l i n e a r} ^ {\prime} \text {e x p a n s i o n}, ^ {\circ} C ^ {- 1},
$$

$$
L = \text {l e n g t h}
$$

Heat shunting factor

$$
\frac {\mathrm {d} \mathrm {F} _ {\mathrm {x}}}{\mathrm {F} _ {\mathrm {x}}} = \frac {\mathrm {d} (1 - \mathrm {F} _ {\mathrm {x}})}{\mathrm {F} _ {\mathrm {x}}} ,
$$

$$
\left(1 - F _ {x}\right) \propto G ^ {0} \cdot^ {3} \propto U _ {2} ^ {0} \cdot^ {3} [ E q. (2 3) ] ,
$$

$$
\frac {\mathrm {d} \left(1 - F _ {x}\right)}{1 - F _ {x}} = 0. 8 \left(\mathrm {d} U _ {2} / U _ {2}\right).
$$

The dominant resistance in the heat-transfer coefficient, $U_{2}$ , measurement is the air gap (Fig. 1). The major uncertainty in determining this resistance is the emissivity. Therefore,

$$
\mathrm {d} U _ {2} / U _ {2} = \mathrm {d} \varepsilon / \varepsilon .
$$

Thus,

$$
\frac {\mathrm {d} F _ {x}}{F _ {x}} = \pm \frac {(1 - F _ {x})}{F _ {x}} \frac {\mathrm {d} (1 - F _ {x})}{1 - F _ {x}} = \pm \frac {(1 - F _ {x})}{F _ {x}} 0. 8 \mathrm {d e} / \epsilon
$$

[ \frac{d\varepsilon}{\varepsilon} = \pm 0.1 \times 100 / 0.4 = \pm 25\% ] (estimated for the air gap). Thus

$$
\mathrm{dF}_{\mathrm{x}} / \mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{x}} = \pm 20(1 - \mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{x}}) / \mathrm{F}_{\mathrm{x}}\% .
$$

Summation of errors

All the uncertainties now combined in Eq. (C-3) yields

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {\mathrm {d k}}{\mathrm {k}} (\%) = \pm 0.013 \left(\frac {1}{x} + \frac {Q ^ {\prime} / A}{k + e h _ {r} x}\right) + \left(\frac {k + e h _ {r} x}{k}\right) \left\{(\pm 0.42) + (\pm 0.2) - (\pm 0.4) \right. \\ - (\pm 0. 3) + \left(\frac {(6 . 3 k _ {\text {a i r}} + 0 . 0 3 h _ {r}) T}{Q ^ {\prime} / A} - 1\right) (\pm 0. 2) + [ \pm 2 0 (1 - F _ {x}) / F _ {x} ] \\ \end{array}
$$

(continued)

$$
\begin{array}{l} + w \frac {k + e h x}{x} [ \pm 2 0 (1 / F _ {x} - 1 / F _ {O}) ] \\ \left. - \frac {\mathrm {e h} _ {\mathrm {r}} ^ {\mathrm {x}}}{\mathrm {k} + \mathrm {e h} _ {\mathrm {r}} ^ {\mathrm {x}}} \left[ (\pm 1 3) + (\pm 0. 6) + (\pm 1 1) \right] \right\}. \\ \end{array}
$$

The standard error in the conductivity was calculated as a function of $x$ , $k$ , and $e h_r$ with $Q' / A = 0.5 \, W \, cm^{-2}$ , $e = 0.5$ , and $w = 20^{\circ}C \, cm^{2} \, W^{-1}$ where the standard error is defined as

$$
\frac {\mathrm {d k}}{\mathrm {k}} = \left[ \sum_ {\mathrm {n}} (\text {e r r o r}) _ {\mathrm {n}} ^ {2} \right] ^ {1 / 2}.
$$

In Fig. C-1, the error calculated using Eqs. (C-3) and (C-4) is shown plotted as a function of the specimen thickness for various values of the specimen conductivity. The error is most sensitive to the magnitude of the specimen conductivity. The primary source of error in the low conductivity measurements at small thicknesses is the uncertainty in the specimen thickness; however, at large thicknesses and for transparent

![](images/e98036b5446e81482e2a56817d95b0c7af5b759e390c30912ed04f50e3c15e51.jpg)  
Fig. C-1. Estimated error in the conductivity measurements for the Apparatus III-B vs specimen thickness at an average specimen temperature of $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for various specimen conductivities. Radiation assumed $(\varepsilon = 0.5, \overline{n} = 1.0, \overline{\kappa} = 0)$ except as noted.

specimens, the uncertainty in the emissivity and index of refraction cause the principal errors. The uncertainty in the temperature measurements comprises the primary source of errors in the high conductivity measurements.

The minimum error from Fig. C-1 occurs in the vicinity of $x = 0.05$ cm. Consequently, most of the experimental data were taken for specimen thicknesses of less than 0.1 cm. A plot of the estimated error limits as a function of specimen conductivity for a specimen thickness of 0.05 cm is shown in Fig. 22, Chapter 6, for the maximum and standard error, where

$$
\left(\mathrm {d k} / \mathrm {k}\right) _ {\max } = \sum_ {\mathrm {n}} (\text {e r r o r}) _ {\mathrm {n}}.
$$
